Create document variables

docvars(inaug_corpus) <- docvars(inaug_corpus) %>%
  as_tibble() %>%
  mutate(
    period = case_when(
      Year < 1900 ~ "Before 1900",
      Year >= 1900 & Year < 1950 ~ "1900-1949",
      Year >= 1950 & Year < 2000 ~ "1950-1999",
      Year >= 2000 ~ "2000-present"
    )
  )

Tokenization and DFM

inaug_tokens <- inaug_corpus %>%
  tokens(remove_punct = TRUE, remove_numbers = TRUE) %>%
  tokens_tolower() %>%
  tokens_remove(stopwords("en"))

inaug_dfm <- dfm(inaug_tokens)

topfeatures(inaug_dfm, 30)

Most frequent words

top_inaug <- textstat_frequency(inaug_dfm, n = 25)

top_inaug %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = reorder(feature, frequency), y = frequency)) +
  geom_col() +
  coord_flip() +
  labs(
    title = "Top Words in US Presidential Inaugural Speeches",
    x = "Word",
    y = "Frequency"
  )

Compare speeches over time

inaug_dfm_period <- dfm_group(inaug_dfm, groups = period)

topfeatures(inaug_dfm_period, 15)
    people government         us        can       must       upon      great 
       592        575        507        489        377        371        354 
       may     states      world     nation    country      shall      every 
       343        343        329        324        323        316        309 
       one 
       274 
freq_period <- textstat_frequency(inaug_dfm_period, groups = docnames(inaug_dfm_period), n = 10)

freq_period %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = reorder(feature, frequency), y = frequency, fill = group)) +
  geom_col(show.legend = FALSE) +
  coord_flip() +
  facet_wrap(~ group, scales = "free") +
  labs(
    title = "Top Words by Historical Period",
    x = "Word",
    y = "Frequency"
  )

Compare selected presidents

selected_presidents <- c("Washington", "Lincoln", "Roosevelt", "Reagan", "Obama", "Trump", "Biden")

inaug_selected <- corpus_subset(inaug_corpus, President %in% selected_presidents)

tokens_selected <- inaug_selected %>%
  tokens(remove_punct = TRUE, remove_numbers = TRUE) %>%
  tokens_tolower() %>%
  tokens_remove(stopwords("en"))

dfm_selected <- dfm(tokens_selected)

topfeatures(dfm_group(dfm_selected, groups = President), 12)
        us     people        can government     nation       must    america 
       163        127        114        101        100         98         90 
       one        now      world      every        new 
        89         75         74         69         62

Interpretation of US Presidential Inaugural Speeches

The analysis of US presidential inaugural speeches reveals both strong continuity and important changes in political language across time and among presidents. Overall, the speeches consistently emphasize themes related to the people, government, national identity, public responsibility, and democratic values.

The overall word frequency results show that the most common terms include “people,” “government,” “us,” “nation,” “country,” “constitution,” “freedom,” and “peace.” This indicates that inaugural speeches consistently focus on collective identity, governance, national unity, and civic ideals. The prominence of words such as “people” and “government” suggests that presidents frequently frame their leadership in relation to public responsibility and democratic legitimacy.

The historical-period comparison shows meaningful shifts in rhetorical emphasis over time. Before 1900, speeches rely more heavily on formal constitutional and institutional language, including words such as “states,” “constitution,” “shall,” and “government.” This reflects the early republic’s focus on constitutional order, federalism, and institution-building.

During the 1900–1949 period, speeches increasingly emphasize international themes and collective responsibility, with prominent words including “world,” “peace,” and “must.” This likely reflects the influence of industrialization, the Great Depression, and the two World Wars on presidential rhetoric.

In the 1950–1999 period, speeches place greater emphasis on “people,” “America,” “world,” and “freedom,” suggesting a stronger focus on public identity, democracy, and global leadership during the Cold War period.

In the 2000-present period, speeches continue to emphasize “people,” “America,” and “nation,” but also show greater use of inclusive and collective language. Compared to earlier speeches, modern inaugurals appear more publicly oriented and emotionally accessible rather than highly constitutional or legalistic.

The word cloud further supports these findings by showing that terms such as “people,” “government,” “country,” “peace,” “freedom,” and “America” dominate the corpus across presidents and historical periods. This demonstrates substantial continuity in the core themes of American political rhetoric.

The document similarity matrix also reveals interesting patterns. For example, the inaugural speeches of Jefferson and Adams show relatively high similarity scores, suggesting comparable rhetorical styles and political concerns during the early republic period. In contrast, Washington’s second inaugural speech has much lower similarity scores with other speeches, likely because it is unusually short and structurally different from most inaugurals. Overall, the similarity matrix suggests that speeches from nearby historical periods often share more similar vocabulary and rhetorical framing.

At the same time, important differences among presidents remain visible. Some presidents emphasize constitutional principles and institutional order, while others focus more heavily on democracy, national unity, public responsibility, or global leadership. These differences likely reflect both individual political styles and the historical challenges facing each administration.

Overall, the analysis suggests that US presidential inaugural speeches combine long-term rhetorical continuity with gradual historical change. Across more than two centuries, presidents consistently invoke themes of nationhood, democracy, and public service, while adapting their language to changing political, economic, and international conditions.

US Department of Defense Reports

Read DOD PDF files

dod_files <- readtext("USDOD/*.pdf")
PDF error: Expected the default config, but wasn't able to find it, or it isn't a Dictionary
dod_texts <- dod_files %>%
  as_tibble() %>%
  clean_names() %>%
  mutate(
    filename = basename(doc_id),
    year = str_extract(filename, "\\d{4}") %>% as.numeric()
  ) %>%
  arrange(year)

head(dod_texts)

docvars(dod_corpus, "year") <- dod_texts$year
docvars(dod_corpus, "filename") <- dod_texts$filename

Compare DOD reports by period

docvars(dod_corpus, "period") <- case_when(
  docvars(dod_corpus, "year") < 2010 ~ "Before 2010",
  docvars(dod_corpus, "year") >= 2010 & docvars(dod_corpus, "year") < 2020 ~ "2010-2019",
  docvars(dod_corpus, "year") >= 2020 ~ "2020-present"
)

dod_tokens_period <- dod_corpus %>%
  tokens(remove_punct = TRUE, remove_numbers = TRUE) %>%
  tokens_tolower() %>%
  tokens_remove(stopwords("en"))

dod_dfm_period <- dfm(dod_tokens_period)
dod_dfm_period_grouped <- dfm_group(dod_dfm_period, groups = period)

topfeatures(dod_dfm_period_grouped, 20)
       china     military      defense      china’s          prc          pla 
        9782         8586         6408         5564         5328         4916 
    security          u.s        force   operations       forces       states 
        4278         3917         3433         3061         3033         2942 
        also     national       united       taiwan          air       report 
        2798         2789         2780         2592         2469         2430 
  technology capabilities 
        2366         2304 
freq_dod_period <- textstat_frequency(
  dod_dfm_period_grouped,
  groups = docnames(dod_dfm_period_grouped),
  n = 12
)

freq_dod_period %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = reorder(feature, frequency), y = frequency)) +
  geom_col() +
  coord_flip() +
  facet_wrap(~ group, scales = "free") +
  labs(
    title = "Top Words in DOD Reports by Period",
    x = "Word",
    y = "Frequency"
  )

Interpretation of DOD Report Analysis

The text analysis of the US Department of Defense China reports reveals several clear patterns in both vocabulary usage and document similarity across time. Overall, the reports consistently focus on China’s military capabilities, strategic development, and national security concerns, while also showing substantial evolution in language and emphasis over time.

The word frequency analysis shows that the most common terms across the corpus include “china,” “military,” “defense,” “prc,” “pla,” and “security.” This indicates that the primary focus of the reports is the assessment of China’s military power and strategic behavior. Other frequently occurring terms such as “technology,” “systems,” “information,” “missile,” “aircraft,” and “Taiwan” suggest that the reports devote increasing attention to military modernization, technological competition, regional security, and cross-strait issues.

The grouped frequency analysis by period demonstrates important changes in emphasis over time. Before 2010, the reports contain relatively more institutional and administrative language, including words such as “committee,” “department,” and “states.” During the 2010–2019 period, terms such as “Taiwan,” “PLA,” and “developments” become more prominent, suggesting growing attention to regional security and Chinese military modernization. In the 2020-present period, words such as “PRC,” “operations,” “people’s,” and “national” become even more dominant, indicating a stronger focus on strategic competition, operational readiness, and long-term national security concerns.

The document similarity matrix also reveals meaningful temporal patterns. The 2002 and 2003 reports have extremely high similarity scores (approximately 0.97), indicating that these reports use very similar language and likely follow highly comparable report structures and strategic framing. Similarly, the 2000 and 2002 reports also show strong similarity (approximately 0.90). In contrast, the 1999 report has substantially lower similarity scores with later reports, suggesting that it differs considerably in vocabulary, structure, and possibly reporting style. This interpretation is also supported by the Wordfish analysis, where the 1999 report appears as a strong outlier.

The Wordfish scaling results show a clear temporal trend in document positions. Earlier reports tend to appear on the negative side of the latent scale, while more recent reports shift progressively toward the positive side. This indicates systematic changes in language usage over time rather than random variation. The feature-position analysis further suggests that the positive side of the scale is associated more strongly with terms related to Taiwan, cyber capabilities, AI, strategic competition, and modernization. Therefore, the estimated latent dimension appears to capture a transition from earlier traditional military reporting toward more technologically and strategically focused language.

The administration-level comparison also reveals clustering patterns. Reports from the Clinton and early Bush years tend to cluster toward the negative side of the Wordfish scale, while reports from the Obama, Trump, Biden, and Trump II periods appear farther toward the positive side. This suggests that the language of DOD reports changes systematically across administrations, although the model does not directly measure aggressiveness or policy effectiveness.

Finally, the comparison between Wordfish and Correspondence Analysis provides strong evidence of robustness. The correlation between the Wordfish theta values and the first Correspondence Analysis dimension is approximately 0.944, indicating that both scaling methods capture highly similar underlying variation in the reports. This strengthens confidence that the observed differences reflect meaningful changes in DOD report language over time rather than artifacts of a single model.

Overall, the analysis demonstrates that DOD China reports have evolved substantially from 1999 to 2025. While all reports consistently focus on military and security issues, more recent reports increasingly emphasize strategic competition, modernization, advanced technology, cyber capabilities, and regional security concerns related to China and Taiwan.

  PATH_DATA,
  pattern = "\\.pdf$",
  full.names = TRUE,
  ignore.case = TRUE
)

cat("Found", length(pdf_files), "PDF files\n")
Found 27 PDF files
read_pdf_safe <- function(filepath) {
  tryCatch({
    pages <- pdftools::pdf_text(filepath)
    text <- paste(pages, collapse = "\n")
    return(text)
  }, error = function(e) {
    warning("Failed to read: ", basename(filepath), " - ", e$message)
    return(NA_character_)
  })
}

DODq <- tibble(
  doc_id = basename(pdf_files),
  text = map_chr(pdf_files, read_pdf_safe)
)
PDF error: Expected the default config, but wasn't able to find it, or it isn't a Dictionary

}

corp_DOD <- corpus(
  DODq,
  text_field = "text",
  docid_field = "doc_id"
)

docvars(corp_DOD, "year") <- DODq$year

docvars(corp_DOD, "president") <- case_when(
  DODq$year <= 2000 ~ "Clinton",
  DODq$year >= 2001 & DODq$year <= 2008 ~ "Bush",
  DODq$year >= 2009 & DODq$year <= 2016 ~ "Obama",
  DODq$year >= 2017 & DODq$year <= 2020 ~ "Trump",
  DODq$year >= 2021 & DODq$year <= 2024 ~ "Biden",
  DODq$year >= 2025 ~ "Trump II",
  TRUE ~ NA_character_
)

toks_DOD <- tokens(
  corp_DOD,
  remove_punct = TRUE,
  remove_numbers = TRUE
) %>%
  tokens_tolower() %>%
  tokens_remove(stopwords("english")) %>%
  tokens_remove(c(
    "page", "figure", "table", "appendix", "chapter",
    "https", "http", "www", "pdf", "gov",
    "department", "defense", "dod"
  ))

dfmat_DOD <- dfm(toks_DOD)

president_positions <- doc_pos %>%
  group_by(president) %>%
  summarize(
    n_reports = n(),
    mean_theta = mean(theta, na.rm = TRUE),
    median_theta = median(theta, na.rm = TRUE),
    sd_theta = sd(theta, na.rm = TRUE),
    min_theta = min(theta, na.rm = TRUE),
    max_theta = max(theta, na.rm = TRUE),
    .groups = "drop"
  ) %>%
  arrange(mean_theta)

)

library(ggrepel)
Warning: package 'ggrepel' was built under R version 4.4.3
scaling_compare <- doc_pos %>%
  left_join(
    dat_ca %>%
      select(document, ca_dim1 = dim1, ca_dim2 = dim2),
    by = "document"
  ) %>%
  filter(!is.na(theta), !is.na(ca_dim1))

cor_wf_ca <- cor(
  scaling_compare$theta,
  scaling_compare$ca_dim1,
  use = "complete.obs"
)

cat("Correlation between Wordfish theta and CA Dimension 1:",
    round(cor_wf_ca, 3), "\n")
Correlation between Wordfish theta and CA Dimension 1: 0.944 
p_scaling_compare <- ggplot(
  scaling_compare,
  aes(x = theta, y = ca_dim1)
) +
  geom_hline(yintercept = 0, linetype = "dotted", color = "gray60") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 0, linetype = "dotted", color = "gray60") +
  geom_smooth(
    method = "lm",
    se = TRUE,
    color = "black",
    linewidth = 0.7
  ) +
  geom_point(
    aes(color = president),
    size = 3,
    alpha = 0.85
  ) +

    max.overlaps = Inf,
    show.legend = FALSE,

    point.padding = 0.2
  ) +
  scale_color_manual(values = pres_colors) +
  labs(

What is Wordfish?

Wordfish is an unsupervised text scaling model that estimates the relative positions of documents based on patterns of word usage. The model assumes that word counts follow a Poisson distribution and identifies a latent dimension that explains variation in language across documents.

Unlike supervised classification methods, Wordfish does not require predefined labels or categories. Instead, it infers document positions directly from word frequencies. Documents that use similar language are positioned closer together, while documents with different language patterns are positioned farther apart.

In political science and policy analysis, Wordfish is commonly used to estimate ideological, strategic, or policy positions from textual data. In this assignment, Wordfish is applied to US Department of Defense reports to examine how the language of DOD China reports changes over time and across presidential administrations.

Wordfish Analysis of DOD Reports

To estimate document positions, I first constructed a document-feature matrix (DFM) from the DOD reports after tokenization and stopword removal. I then trimmed the DFM to remove extremely rare and extremely common terms, since these words add noise and reduce the stability of the model.

Next, I applied the `textmodel_wordfish()` function from the `quanteda.textmodels` package. Because the direction of the Wordfish scale is arbitrary, I selected an early report and a recent report as anchor documents using the `dir` argument. This makes the scale easier to interpret as movement from earlier DOD report language toward more recent DOD report language.

Interpretation of Wordfish Results

The Wordfish analysis reveals substantial variation in the language of DOD China reports across time. The estimated document positions show a strong temporal pattern, with reports becoming progressively more positive on the latent Wordfish scale from 1999 to 2025. This suggests that the vocabulary and strategic emphasis of the reports change systematically over time rather than fluctuating randomly.

One particularly important result is that the 1999 report appears as a strong outlier. Its estimated theta value is substantially lower than all other reports, and it is also much longer than the rest of the corpus. This suggests that the report differs not only in substantive content but also in structure, formatting, and vocabulary usage. Therefore, some of the variation captured by Wordfish may reflect differences in report style and document construction in addition to strategic language.

The administration-level comparison also shows meaningful clustering patterns. Reports from the Clinton and early Bush years tend to appear on the more negative side of the scale, while reports from the Obama, Trump, Biden, and Trump II years tend to appear farther toward the positive side. This indicates that reports produced under later administrations use systematically different language patterns compared to earlier reports.

The feature-position plot helps interpret the latent dimension estimated by Wordfish. Terms such as “Taiwan,” “AI,” “cyber,” “space,” and “competition” are associated more strongly with the positive side of the scale. This suggests that the estimated dimension may capture a shift from earlier traditional military reporting toward more technologically and strategically focused language emphasizing strategic competition, modernization, and regional security concerns.

The Correspondence Analysis results provide additional support for the Wordfish findings. The correlation between the Wordfish theta values and the first Correspondence Analysis dimension is approximately 0.944, indicating that both scaling methods capture highly similar underlying variation in the DOD reports. This strengthens confidence that the observed patterns reflect meaningful differences in report language rather than artifacts of a single model.

At the same time, several limitations should be considered. First, the analysis includes only 27 reports, which may reduce the stability of the estimated latent scale. Second, PDF extraction may introduce formatting or OCR noise, especially in earlier reports. Finally, Wordfish identifies latent language differences but does not directly measure concepts such as aggressiveness, hostility, or policy effectiveness. Therefore, the results should be interpreted as evidence of changing language patterns and strategic emphasis rather than direct measures of policy orientation.

How to Compare Positions

Wordfish compares document positions by estimating a latent dimension from word frequency patterns. Each document receives a position parameter called theta, which represents its location on the estimated scale. Documents with similar theta values use similar language and are interpreted as having similar strategic or policy-related language patterns, while documents with very different theta values are interpreted as emphasizing different themes or priorities.

Because the direction of the Wordfish scale is arbitrary, anchor documents are used to define the direction of interpretation. In this analysis, an early DOD report and a recent DOD report were selected as anchor documents. This makes the scale easier to interpret as movement from earlier DOD reporting language toward more recent reporting language.

Positions can be compared in several ways. First, the one-dimensional Wordfish plot allows direct comparison of individual reports along the latent scale. Second, grouping documents by presidential administration helps identify whether reports cluster differently across administrations. Third, plotting theta values over time allows researchers to examine long-term trends and changes in strategic language.

To evaluate whether the Wordfish positions are robust, Correspondence Analysis (CA) can also be used as an alternative scaling method. Unlike Wordfish, which estimates a single latent dimension, Correspondence Analysis can represent documents across multiple dimensions based on their word distributions. If both methods produce similar document patterns, this increases confidence that the observed differences are substantively meaningful rather than artifacts of a single model.

In this analysis, the correlation between the Wordfish theta values and the first Correspondence Analysis dimension is approximately 0.944, indicating that both scaling methods capture highly similar variation in the DOD reports. This suggests that the estimated document positions are relatively stable and reflect systematic differences in report language over time.

Conclusion

Overall, the Wordfish and Correspondence Analysis results suggest that the language of DOD China reports has evolved substantially over time. Earlier reports focus more on traditional military concerns, while more recent reports increasingly emphasize strategic competition, technology, cyber capabilities, and regional security issues.

These findings demonstrate how scaling methods in text analytics can reveal latent structure and policy positioning in large collections of political and strategic documents.
